    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5974152802706152781">Pather Panchali deals with the life of the Roy family, consisting of Harihar, Sarbajaya, Apu and Durga, both in their ancestral village Nishchindipur in rural Bengal and later when they move to Varanasi in search of a better life, as well as the anguish and loss they face during their travels.<br/><br/>It first appeared as a serial in a Calcutta periodical in 1928 and was published </span>
  <span id="freeText5974152802706152781" style="display:none">Pather Panchali deals with the life of the Roy family, consisting of Harihar, Sarbajaya, Apu and Durga, both in their ancestral village Nishchindipur in rural Bengal and later when they move to Varanasi in search of a better life, as well as the anguish and loss they face during their travels.<br /><br />It first appeared as a serial in a Calcutta periodical in 1928 and was published as a book the next year; it was the first published novel written by the author. It was followed in 1932 by a sequel Aparajito, which was later also adapted into a film of the same name by Satyajit Ray.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5974152802706152781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_6966788" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6966788" />
    <a title="Tia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87324-tia"><img alt="Tia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1185082493p2/87324.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6966788?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tia" href="/user/show/87324-tia">Tia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Satyajit Ray devotees, and those who like the slow village life</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87324-tia?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87324-tia?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6966788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12322214246822336167">This is a somewhat slow, silent story of a young boy growing up in a poor village in Bengal. It follows Apu and Durga, (two children who are poor even by the standards of their village mates), as they search for wild berries and leaves, steal mangoes from their neighbors, get beaten, play cowries, get lost, and discover trains. Their lives are simple and their stories simply told. You get a sense both of the rarified, natural beauty that village life holds, and of the ways in which it grinds peo</span>
  <span id="freeText12322214246822336167" style="display:none">This is a somewhat slow, silent story of a young boy growing up in a poor village in Bengal. It follows Apu and Durga, (two children who are poor even by the standards of their village mates), as they search for wild berries and leaves, steal mangoes from their neighbors, get beaten, play cowries, get lost, and discover trains. Their lives are simple and their stories simply told. You get a sense both of the rarified, natural beauty that village life holds, and of the ways in which it grinds people down, keeps them narrow, and strips them of their dreams. <br /><br />The other interesting thing about the story is how tangential British presence is in the story. We hear about a now defunct factory near the village, we see the train tracks, and we see a somewhat ridiculous Westernized family putting on airs. But ultimately, the world they live in is so small that Shorbojoya, the mother of Apu and Durga, has never been to a temple 6 miles away in the 10 years she lived in the village. It is a kind of pre-Colonial life that could have been described almost identically if it had been set in the 1600's. <br /><br />Durga, who is constantly derided, often beaten, never quite loved -- is especially heartbreaking. Because she is a girl, she never gets as much food, is berated for not doing housework, is never taught to read, and lives a jungly, solitary existence. In other ways, the story plainly captures the ways in which women were powerless, less valued, and utterly mistreated.<br /><br />This story was adapted into a famous Satyajit Ray movie. In some ways I like the movie better, because film seems a better medium to capture the twittering, dripping sounds of the forest, or the quiet, long shots of fields of waving grass. <br /><br />Still, I would recommend reading the book because it captures the way a child sees the world in a particularly charming and unpretentious manner.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12322214246822336167" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_942705132" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/942705132" />
    <a title="Shashini Vidanapathirana Abeysinghe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23621066-shashini-vidanapathirana-abeysinghe"><img alt="Shashini Vidanapathirana Abeysinghe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378616669p2/23621066.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/942705132?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shashini Vidanapathirana Abeysinghe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shashini Vidanapathirana Abeysinghe" href="/user/show/23621066-shashini-vidanapathirana-abeysinghe">Shashini Vidanapathirana Abeysinghe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9556520277" href="/book/show/12370974">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer942705132" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4177820441357152019">This book started my love for Indian novels. The apu's trilogy, Aranakata Pem banda and Gora,Srikantha, Srikantha and Rajalakshmi, all books translated by Ms Sinhaarachchi were loved and read and re read until they got rabbit eared. I was just a teen, eager to get lost in a world, and forget that I was seated in a corner of the school library, unseen by anyone.<br/>The story takes you to world of a bygone era, where Apu and his sister love each other with the same love-hate passion that is common for</span>
  <span id="freeText4177820441357152019" style="display:none">This book started my love for Indian novels. The apu's trilogy, Aranakata Pem banda and Gora,Srikantha, Srikantha and Rajalakshmi, all books translated by Ms Sinhaarachchi were loved and read and re read until they got rabbit eared. I was just a teen, eager to get lost in a world, and forget that I was seated in a corner of the school library, unseen by anyone.<br />The story takes you to world of a bygone era, where Apu and his sister love each other with the same love-hate passion that is common for all Era's. We find what it means to be poor, we empathize with Sarwajaya, her harsh life, the little beauties of life she cherishes that she get through her children. Mostly we see a girl, old enough to be married, but yet an adventurous dreamer who has discovered the beauty of the life through little sweet berries and little secret treasures of nature known only to a wandering soul like her. She asks, from her little brother, with eyes full of hope, whether he would take her to see the railway. Her wish never fulfilled, her thirst for life unstated, we bid her good bye with a tear. It maybe because I was just a teenager, with an active imagination and a soft heart without much exposure to the rueful world. But these stories shook me to my very core and left it's mark and has such a powerful hold of me to this date, that while writing this even, a tear came to my eyes just remembering the initial painful moment where I cried with Apu over the loss of his dearest big sister.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4177820441357152019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2369034094" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2369034094" />
    <a title="Smitha Murthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3547543-smitha-murthy"><img alt="Smitha Murthy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585489620p2/3547543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2369034094?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Smitha Murthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Smitha Murthy" href="/user/show/3547543-smitha-murthy">Smitha Murthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788172233334" href="/book/show/1391179.Pather_Panchali">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Smitha by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2992522-swati">Swati</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=india-translated">india-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=family">family</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2369034094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9684195858196886904">I read this to celebrate my friend’s birthday week. I haven’t read much of Bengali literature, and my friend had long since urged me to read Bibhutibhushan’s works. What an alliteration-filled name! I had desisted reading this saying that I can’t even pronounce the author’s name. <br/><br/>But finally, I did come around. ‘Pather Panchali’ was gifted by a friend, and it was lying there on my bookshelf. When I did start reading, I fell in love with Opu and Durga. Bandyopadhyay at once took me on a journey t</span>
  <span id="freeText9684195858196886904" style="display:none">I read this to celebrate my friend’s birthday week. I haven’t read much of Bengali literature, and my friend had long since urged me to read Bibhutibhushan’s works. What an alliteration-filled name! I had desisted reading this saying that I can’t even pronounce the author’s name. <br /><br />But finally, I did come around. ‘Pather Panchali’ was gifted by a friend, and it was lying there on my bookshelf. When I did start reading, I fell in love with Opu and Durga. Bandyopadhyay at once took me on a journey to the heart of rural Bengal. Here at once is a depiction of poverty in terms of material wealth, but the lives of Opu and Durga come alive with richness. The descriptive flair of the narration had me enthralled and the antics of Opu and Durga tugged at my heartstrings. <br /><br />Of such is the flavor of life - the cycle of longing, desire, loss, and the cycle of finding happiness in the smallest things. Bandyopadhyay excels in creating a world that you can immediately immerse yourself in, and he does know how to get inside a child’s head. Easily one of my favorite books with an ending that left me in tears. Thank you @Swati for pushing me to read this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9684195858196886904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2371268409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2371268409" />
    <a title="Darren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51183187-darren"><img alt="Darren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454936698p2/51183187.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2371268409?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darren" href="/user/show/51183187-darren">Darren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51183187-darren?shelf=world">world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51183187-darren?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51183187-darren?shelf=zz-pc-2019-52-few">zz-pc-2019-52-few</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2371268409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5201820633349396709">Simple portrayal of the day-to-day life of a poor Bengali family in the early years of 20th century, mainly from the POV of the two children Opu and Durga. Although very little happens, we get to know the characters so well (their motivations, hopes, dreams etc, esp Opu's love of the natural world) that they practically coalesce into reality before the reader's eyes. Deeply affecting/moving masterpiece.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

But this novel contented with children's world without much major social or political chaos. Another important thing, there is no solid story or proper handling of time. The novel is about the rural domestic Bengali village life correlating a boy's childhood with his family in a very simple language. Author's command over children's psychology is outstanding.   <span id="freeText1912922277329104025" style="display:none">I wish I could read Bengali !!. This is the Indian equivalent of Harper Lee's <i>To Kill a Mockingbird</i>. But this novel contented with children's world without much major social or political chaos. Another important thing, there is no solid story or proper handling of time. The novel is about the rural domestic Bengali village life correlating a boy's childhood with his family in a very simple language. Author's command over children's psychology is outstanding. I hope for another better English translation.</span>

<div id="review_438532007" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/438532007" />
    <a title="Harsh Vivek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13818741-harsh-vivek"><img alt="Harsh Vivek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350807634p2/13818741.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/438532007?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harsh Vivek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harsh Vivek" href="/user/show/13818741-harsh-vivek">Harsh Vivek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer438532007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14410664885163716913">A very touching story of the lives of ordinary Indians under excruciating poverty in the pre-independence Bengal. In a very simple, yet powerful language, the author has woven an intricate web of human emotions, expressed through the eyes of two beautiful young children. The description of the life of Bengal in those days is near flawless. It is often said that poverty cannot be explained - it has to be experienced, and this book stands tall as a testimony to it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3403531784&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3403531784" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16660820407647513861"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://link.medium.com/QvYetGdujab'>https://link.medium.com/QvYetGdujab</a> <br/><br/>I read this recently, and thoughts about the book has been stuck with me since then. Although the movie based on the book, directed by Satyajit Ray, is much more popular and acclaimed, I have no intention of watching it. I do not want the images that this book has planted in my mind be distorted. And I imagine the original book as written in Bengali would have been more beautiful.<br/><br/>The first few chapters of the book revolves around an old lady, Inder Thakrun. In</span>
  <span id="freeText16660820407647513861" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://link.medium.com/QvYetGdujab">https://link.medium.com/QvYetGdujab</a> <br /><br />I read this recently, and thoughts about the book has been stuck with me since then. Although the movie based on the book, directed by Satyajit Ray, is much more popular and acclaimed, I have no intention of watching it. I do not want the images that this book has planted in my mind be distorted. And I imagine the original book as written in Bengali would have been more beautiful.<br /><br />The first few chapters of the book revolves around an old lady, Inder Thakrun. Inder has nobody to look after her and manages to live off her distant relative — Horihor. The daughter-in-law of the family, Shorobojya, forces her out. Inder goes to another village to find her long lost son-in-law who is living with his bunch of grown up children and grandchildren. Every household that is described in the story has a young widowed person. Without much conversation to make with these folks she goes back “home” where she is reluctantnly given space. Her one friend is the charming girl child in the house — Durga. Inder scrapes for food but when she is served something she makes sure it is relished. Like the one time she is eating rice with a hungry Durga watching every grain of rice making its way into Inder’s mouth. The reader is made to expect Inder to offer the child some of it, as much as Durga herself. But Inder only has the stomach, literally, to offer Durga half a banana at the end of it.<br /><br />Inevitably Inder is once again sent out of the house by Shorobojya and this time she dies in the middle of a road out of the village while going nowhere. While the story of Inder is tragic, the narration is so well detailed that you always knew that only one outcome is possible for poor Inder. And so you find humour in her exchanges and in her story.<br /><br />The story moves on to revolve around Durga and her younger brother Opu. Durga spends her days wandering the village, looking for scrap vegetables &amp; fruits from trees of other houses, gets branded a thief by pretty much the entire village; sometimes she is physically hit for her mischief, is served some food only after Opu is well fed, gets treated like a burden all the time by her mother and yet you get the feeling she is loved by every creature alike — the ants and the animals in the book, all the human characters, the author and most of all — the reader. Her demise is, without doubt, the most painful death of a fictional character ever for me.<br /><br />Picture this. Cooking rice and raw brinjal fry in the middle of the woods on a beautiful summer afternoon was probably the best day of their (Opu and Durga’s) childhood years. And to think they forgot the salt.<br />“Life is hard, but it stores little thrills and joys along its many curves and bends. They wait for us in the middle of mundane days, anointed with the light of unexpected happiness.”<br />This is the scene that will forever stay in my head when I think about this book.<br /><br />On Durga’s death this is what Bibhutibhushan Bandopadhyay conjured up.<br />“Sometimes, eternity beckons us through familiar blue skies. Children with too much spirit and curiosity for this rule-bound world answer that call eagerly, diving into the deep blue of an unending beyond. The paths of that place are beyond mortal understanding, for they eschew the conventions and rules we hold so dear. Durga’s restless, unruly, inquisitive soul had heard that call, and seen it as her biggest, most wonderful adventure. She had probably yielded her mortal life with gladness.”<br /><br />The story then revolves around Opu. With his father, Horihor, seemingly settled at Kashi, Opu and his mother are destined to move out of the village. “The pinnacle of his success was going to be making rounds of the village on puja days, conducting rituals for food and a pittance. He was still a boy who begged his mother for reading oil at night and got told off.”<br /><br />“His fantasies functioned on the principle that everything would automatically start happening once he was ‘older’. Opportunities for getting ahead would be strewn all around him; it was just a matter of time till he found them. Once he became a grown-up, invitations from all over would issue forth for him. And he would finally be able to set out on his quest — to know all of the world, and the people that lived in it.”<br /><br />As they plot their journey to Kashi, it obviously means a visit to the temple of Goddess Shiddheshwori which was ten kilometers away. Our man of the household, Opu, bravely takes up this journey by foot. His sights, sounds, smell described along this journey will also live with me for the rest of my life.<br /><br />“Opu’s bare feet had become caked with a thin layer of warm, sandy soil. It made his feet toasty and comfortable.”<br />Reading this, do you even want to wear shoes anymore?<br /><br />“Opu had been reared in the lap of rural nature. Here, the change of seasons always sent word ahead of itself — through trees, the winds, the sky and birdsong. The magnificent canvas of this riverine land had taught him to know instinctively what the first subtle changes in the surrounding trees, air and flowing water meant. This harmony was something he loved and cherished, and he couldn’t imagine a life bereft of it. The shimmering heat of summer, the gradual gathering of deep blue, solemn, serious clouds across the horizon at the end of the hot months, the magical sunset colours above the Shonadanga moors, the uninterrupted stretch of white kans-grass flowers from here to Madhobpur as the month of Bhadro came to an end, the webs of moonlight that filtered through leaves on the golden full-moon nights, and wove patterns on the village roads . . . all of these had found an eternal home in Opu’s young mind. From birth to boyhood, they had trained his senses in the grace and splendour of everyday life, had whispered chants of the infinite into his eager ears. Without him realizing it, freedom — in its aspect as untrammelled nature — had instilled in him an adoration of the pure and the beautiful. It was a devotion that would blossom within him for the rest of his life.”<br /><br />Opu’s friendship with Gulki and his first train journey to Kashi are to be cherished.<br /><br />Life gets incredibly hard at Kashi but we are talking about Opu here. I am sure he gets transformed into a man molded by his innocence, experience and with Durga’s spirit. As the God of the Travelled Road tells him at the end “When I first made you homeless, I also anointed you with the invisible mark of this strange, joyous, never-ending road. There is no going back on this road, only forward. So come, my child. Let us go forth.”<br /><br />I shed many a tear through this book and I will recommend this to every person I meet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16660820407647513861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15318254530015198161">A series of vignettes originally written in Bengali. Bibhutibhushan Banjeri was a writer in the early 20th century and wrote fictionalized stories of his life in a village in Bengal. His stories were turned into trilogy of movies in the 1950s by Satyajit Ray. I was surprised to discover the original stories were written in the 1910s. The stories are more scattered because they were intended to be in a novel. The family in the stories are from an ancient Brahmin family who has fallen in hard time</span>
  <span id="freeText15318254530015198161" style="display:none">A series of vignettes originally written in Bengali. Bibhutibhushan Banjeri was a writer in the early 20th century and wrote fictionalized stories of his life in a village in Bengal. His stories were turned into trilogy of movies in the 1950s by Satyajit Ray. I was surprised to discover the original stories were written in the 1910s. The stories are more scattered because they were intended to be in a novel. The family in the stories are from an ancient Brahmin family who has fallen in hard times. The father can’t make a living from from his tenants and religious practices. He had an education mainly in reading but while he can use it to teach his son Bengali and to read the ancient stories. He doesn’t know math, English, or other subjects. The eldest who is a daughter is hungry and lost most of the time. She steals trinkets and food from the neighbors and eventually gets seriously ill with malaria. An elderly family member is treated harshly and rejected by them. The mother feels harshly against everyone while the house steadily falls apart. The husband is gone often trying to make money. A dark story in which the boy uses what education he has to go to school and escape. </span>

<div id="review_3333997374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3333997374" />
    <a title="SuchismitaGhosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/115123907-suchismitaghosh"><img alt="SuchismitaGhosh" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3333997374?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="SuchismitaGhosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="SuchismitaGhosh" href="/user/show/115123907-suchismitaghosh">SuchismitaGhosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3333997374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7651250034320100112">Pather Panchali is a masterpiece written by <br/>Bhibhutibhushan Banerjee. This story is about the child <br/>protagonist Apu and his sister and the myriad of events that they <br/>become a part of , over and over a period of his life which ends <br/>with the death of his sister. It is a beautiful flow of emotions as <br/>written by the author.<br/>It was first published in 1928 then again as a whole complete <br/>version in 1929. The story is basically about <br/>the child protagonist Apu whose narration is somewhat related <br/>to the au</span>
  <span id="freeText7651250034320100112" style="display:none">Pather Panchali is a masterpiece written by <br />Bhibhutibhushan Banerjee. This story is about the child <br />protagonist Apu and his sister and the myriad of events that they <br />become a part of , over and over a period of his life which ends <br />with the death of his sister. It is a beautiful flow of emotions as <br />written by the author.<br />It was first published in 1928 then again as a whole complete <br />version in 1929. The story is basically about <br />the child protagonist Apu whose narration is somewhat related <br />to the author's own childhood. "Pather" means the path or <br />journey while "Panchali" means a folk poem or song, which can <br />be given a name as "Song of the Road". The story also glides <br />like a river that's flowing in a pleasant manner with the path that <br />has been beautifully described. The story was originally written <br />in Bengali.<br />The plot-<br /><br />The plot is set in the village of Nischindpur where the family of Apu lives in an ancestral home of their grandmother Indir Thakrun who happens to be the aunt of Apu's father Harihor Rai who is a priest but not a man of practical affairs. He lives in a dreamland of his own and thinks of making big in life someday through his poems and scholarly writings. He is projected as a weak person when it comes to earning a good living to lead the family.<br /><br />So it comes to the responsibility of Apu's mother Sarbhojaya who takes care of the whole family and tries hard to run the family with whatever is left in the house.She has been portrayed in a more positive light when it comes to the practical affairs and she also is a woman of high self-esteem as there are several instances where she refuses to ask for monetary help from her relatives who stay in the same village. <br /><br />Another important person in this story is Indir Thakurun who has lost her whole family and is forced to live alone. So she accepts the proposal of letting in Harihor and their family members who were far off relatives to her. She is beautifully moulded in the plot to describe the feelings of elderly people and response of children versus grown-ups towards them.<br /> <br />While Sarbhojaya is overly offensive on the old lady with whom they are forced to share their rations (although they are living in the old lady's home) Durga, her daughter and Apu's elder sister is sympathetic and has a filial affinity towards the old lady. Whenever the old lady leaves the house, unable to bear the indifferent attitude by Sarbhojaya, it is Durga who drags her grandma back to the home, with her innocent talk. After a few days she passes away due to ill health and old age. <br /><br />Durga is the free-spirited girl who refuses to be never bogged down by the difficulties and is presented as a cheerful and happy girl. She doesn't mind taking away the things which she likes very much such as mangoes from trees of other family people of the village; the bead necklace from her neighbours etc. But she has a good and kind heart. It is just that poverty and failure to fulfill her desires forces her to steal them.<br /><br />Apu is more of an innocent kid who has followed in his father's footsteps as it can be seen that he is more interested in the scholarly books and articles that are brought home by his father and is more happy to be left alone writing poems and plays of his own, unlike his sister who is always bubbling and easily approaches any person with whom she wishes to interact. He is more of a shy kid who is driven by her sister's actions. Together they share the simplest of joys which any young brother and sister can have, like eating secretly the stolen mangoes from neighbour's backyard, running after a candyman who passes through the village, walking miles far away from their home just to have a glimpse of the train and so on. The description is so beautiful that we are transported to the place where the kids enjoy and have fun. He prefers solitude and starts writing poems and plays of his own. He is shown as a kid who come to perform in their village during the time of Dussehra. He grows more sober as compared to instinctive and child-like behavior of Durga.<br /><br />As Harihor fails to make any considerable income from the village, he decides to try his luck elsewhere. He promises Sarbhojaya to come back soon with lots of money and gifts for kids. Although he goes here and there, his search goes in vain for the major part. So occasionally he sends a small token amount home which is not at all sufficient to run the family chores. They fall into greater poverty in this period. In addition to that Durga falls ill and cyclone hits them at the same time. Due to lack of medical facilities in the nearby village and monetary problems she fails to fully recover from the fever and on a stormy night the dilapidated house collapses partially and also takes away the life of Durga.<br /><br />Finally Harihor gets a good opportunity as a family priest of a rich family. Brimmed with joy and unaware of the recent happenings in his house he comes back to inform them of the new job and and brings with him new gifts for the family.<br /><br /><br />The book comes to an end as they board a train that takes them away from this troubled land and to their new destination where they dream of leading a better life. All this while Apu keeps thinking of his sister who always wanted to see and board a train.<br /><br /> Lastly, I would like to write that, it left me in tears as the last page came to an end. The story is a simple expression of human interactions and emotions that has been presented beautifully. Even a movie was made in the name "Song of the Little Road" by legendary director Satyajit Ray which was his directorial debut. Bibutibhushan Banerjee brought another book "Aparjita" as a sequel to this novel, which remained a bit unsuccessful unlike the original book which remained the biggest success in his literary career.<br />Trust readers, trust the writer, most importantly, trust the text, the words, the integrity. Last but not the least, I would like to say that I would definitely be recommending this famous and outstanding novel to my friends and families and to everyone out there .<br /><br />-Suchismita Ghosh.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7651250034320100112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3333997374">
  <td colspan="2">

  <span id="freeText2805726476102066681" style="display:none">Always difficult to review a classic which has been feted and dissected. Merit &amp; technicalities take a backseat. The emotional coherence of the story persisted inspite of (seemingly) disjointed scenes.<br /> <br />As with all great literature, if a reader feels for its protagonist, it is a success.               >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17953203767996901565">The best book I have read so far in my life. The raw emotion in the sentences are so powerful. The characters are etched in my mind very powerfully. I had to stop reading the book towards the end because I could not gather the emotional stability to read on, such is the power in the words. I finally finished the book after laying off for a week. Unbelievable writing! I will never forget Durga and Opu.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gQsiVsrh6QBPycE2NYVQq1odxT+SQd++oogF3mKGpOWDWhyBdAd2H/C8SrP9PKL4ZptmPpAQwKoTIt4DT2MzFg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fx7V8xQnfxEhqFlPjZrdeFkxrE2o9GhhlKl1CAhJgxR9T+skqsHgDp7d0spFIy8rZbcPTKqld3UlA67VJawU5w==')})
  }

</script>

When Devdas returns to his village, now a handsome lad of nineteen, Paro asks him to marry her. But Devdas is unable to stand up to parental opposition to the match and rejects the proposition. Stunned, Paro agrees to marry an elderly widower. Devdas returns to Calcutta, but every waking hour of his is now filled with thoughts of Paro and his unfulfilled love for her. Desperate to resolve the situation somehow, he runs to Paro who is now married and asks her to elope with him, but she refuses.\n\nHeartbroken, he seeks solace in alcohol and in the company of the courtesan Chandramukhi. Chandramukhi falls in love with Devdas, but even when he is with her he can only think of Paro. It is now his destiny to hurtle on relentlessly on the path to self-destruction. Devdas’s tortured life ends when, dying of a liver ailment brought on by alcoholism, he journeys to Paro’s house to see her one last time. Arriving in the middle of the night, he dies unknown, untended, on her doorstep. Paro comes to know of his death only the following morning. Devdas has enthralled readers and filmgoing audiences alike for the better part of a century. As a child, he idealizes the chaste Annada Didi—the epitome of selfless devotion to a worthless husband... As a young man he travels to Burma looking for new experiences and meets the rebellious Abhaya—who rejects her violent, bigamous husband to live openly with her lover—and learns to question the hypocritical social norms that bind a woman down but let a man off. He experiments with becoming a sanyasi, is bewitched for a while by the Vaishnavi, Kamal Lata, and wanders on till his directionless existence finally finds a focus—when he resigns himself to life with the notorious but stunning Pyari Baiji, breaking free of the social values he grew up with.\n\nThrough his dynamic and arresting characters, Saratchandra brings alive nineteenth-century Bengal, a prejudice-ridden society that needed to be radically changed.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6093739.Bibhutibhushan_Bandyopadhyay">About Bibhutibhushan Bandyopadhyay</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2180137898769260001"><b>Bibhutibhushan Bandyopadhyay</b> (Bangla: বিভূতিভূষণ বন্দ্যোপাধ্যায়) was an Indian Bangali author and one of the leading writers of modern Bangla literature. His best known work is the autobiographical novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/617097.Pather_Panchali__Song_of_the_Road' title='Pather Panchali: Song of the Road' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pather Panchali: Song of the Road</a> which was later adapted (along with Aparajito, the sequel) into the Apu Trilogy films, directed by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6590819.Satyajit_Ray' title='Satyajit Ray' rel='nofollow noopener'>Satyajit Ray</a>.<br/><br/>The 1951 Rabindra Puraskar, the most prestigious</span>
  <span id="freeText2180137898769260001" style="display:none"><b>Bibhutibhushan Bandyopadhyay</b> (Bangla: বিভূতিভূষণ বন্দ্যোপাধ্যায়) was an Indian Bangali author and one of the leading writers of modern Bangla literature. His best known work is the autobiographical novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/617097.Pather_Panchali__Song_of_the_Road" title="Pather Panchali: Song of the Road" rel="nofollow noopener">Pather Panchali: Song of the Road</a> which was later adapted (along with Aparajito, the sequel) into the Apu Trilogy films, directed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6590819.Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray" rel="nofollow noopener">Satyajit Ray</a>.<br /><br />The 1951 Rabindra Puraskar, the most prestigious literary award in the West Bengal state of India, was posthumously awarded to Bibhutibhushan for his novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15721609._______" title=" ইছামতী" rel="nofollow noopener"> ইছামতী</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2180137898769260001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
